{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_011zTGj485oZ7KGsrL6itHfP","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→A value was moved out while it was still borrowed.\n     2→\n     3→Erroneous code example:\n     4→\n     5→```compile_fail,E0505\n     6→struct Value {}\n     7→\n     8→fn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n     9→\n    10→fn eat(val: Value) {}\n    11→\n    12→fn main() {\n    13→    let x = Value{};\n    14→    let _ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    15→    eat(x);\n    16→    borrow(_ref_to_val);\n    17→}\n    18→```\n    19→\n    20→Here, the function `eat` takes ownership of `x`. However,\n    21→`x` cannot be moved because the borrow to `_ref_to_val`\n    22→needs to last till the function `borrow`.\n    23→To fix that you can do a few different things:\n    24→\n    25→* Try to avoid moving the variable.\n    26→* Release borrow before move.\n    27→* Implement the `Copy` trait on the type.\n    28→\n    29→Examples:\n    30→\n    31→```\n    32→struct Value {}\n    33→\n    34→fn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n    35→\n    36→fn eat(val: &Value) {}\n    37→\n    38→fn main() {\n    39→    let x = Value{};\n    40→\n    41→    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    42→    eat(&x); // pass by reference, if it's possible\n    43→    borrow(ref_to_val);\n    44→}\n    45→```\n    46→\n    47→Or:\n    48→\n    49→```\n    50→struct Value {}\n    51→\n    52→fn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n    53→\n    54→fn eat(val: Value) {}\n    55→\n    56→fn main() {\n    57→    let x = Value{};\n    58→\n    59→    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    60→    borrow(ref_to_val);\n    61→    // ref_to_val is no longer used.\n    62→    eat(x);\n    63→}\n    64→```\n    65→\n    66→Or:\n    67→\n    68→```\n    69→#[derive(Clone, Copy)] // implement Copy trait\n    70→struct Value {}\n    71→\n    72→fn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n    73→\n    74→fn eat(val: Value) {}\n    75→\n    76→fn main() {\n    77→    let x = Value{};\n    78→    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    79→    eat(x); // it will be copied here.\n    80→    borrow(ref_to_val);\n    81→}\n    82→```\n    83→\n    84→For more information on Rust's ownership system, take a look at the\n    85→[References & Borrowing][references-and-borrowing] section of the Book.\n    86→\n    87→[references-and-borrowing]: https://doc.rust-lang.org/book/ch04-02-references-and-borrowing.html\n    88→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. However,\n`x` cannot be moved because the borrow to `_ref_to_val`\nneeds to last till the function `borrow`.\nTo fix that you can do a few different things:\n\n* Try to avoid moving the variable.\n* Release borrow before move.\n* Implement the `Copy` trait on the type.\n\nExamples:\n\n```\nstruct Value {}\n\nfn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n\nfn eat(val: &Value) {}\n\nfn main() {\n    let x = Value{};\n\n    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    eat(&x); // pass by reference, if it's possible\n    borrow(ref_to_val);\n}\n```\n\nOr:\n\n```\nstruct Value {}\n\nfn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n\nfn eat(val: Value) {}\n\nfn main() {\n    let x = Value{};\n\n    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    borrow(ref_to_val);\n    // ref_to_val is no longer used.\n    eat(x);\n}\n```\n\nOr:\n\n```\n#[derive(Clone, Copy)] // implement Copy trait\nstruct Value {}\n\nfn borrow(val: &Value) {}\n\nfn eat(val: Value) {}\n\nfn main() {\n    let x = Value{};\n    let ref_to_val: &Value = &x;\n    eat(x); // it will be copied here.\n    borrow(ref_to_val);\n}\n```\n\nFor more information on Rust's ownership system, take a look at the\n[References & Borrowing][references-and-borrowing] section of the Book.\n\n[references-and-borrowing]: https://doc.rust-lang.org/book/ch04-02-references-and-borrowing.html\n","numLines":88,"startLine":1,"totalLines":88}}}

The borrowck rules for proving disjointness are applied from the \"root\" of the\n     2→//! borrow forwards, iterating over \"similar\" projections in lockstep until\n     3→//! we can prove overlap one way or another. Essentially, we treat `Overlap` as\n     4→//! a monoid and report a conflict if the product ends up not being `Disjoint`.\n     5→//!\n     6→//! On each step, if we didn't run out of borrow or place, we know that our elements\n     7→//! have the same type, and that they only overlap if they are the identical.\n     8→//!\n     9→//! For example, if we are comparing these:\n    10→//! ```text\n    11→//! BORROW:  (*x1[2].y).z.a\n    12→//! ACCESS:  (*x1[i].y).w.b\n    13→//! ```\n    14→//!\n    15→//! (*x1[2].y).z | (*x1[i].y).w  -- we are disjoint and don't need to check more!\n    22→//! ```\n    23→//!\n    24→//! Because `zip` does potentially bad things to the iterator inside, this loop\n    25→//! also handles the case where the access might be a *prefix* of the borrow, e.g.\n    26→//!\n    27→//! ```text\n    28→//! BORROW:  (*x1[2].y).z.a\n    29→//! ACCESS:  x1[i].y\n    30→//! ```\n    31→//!\n    32→//! The borrowck rules for proving disjointness are applied from the \"root\" of the\n//! borrow forwards, iterating over \"similar\" projections in lockstep until\n//! we can prove overlap one way or another. Essentially, we treat `Overlap` as\n//! a monoid and report a conflict if the product ends up not being `Disjoint`.\n//!\n//! On each step, if we didn't run out of borrow or place, we know that our elements\n//! have the same type, and that they only overlap if they are the identical.\n//!\n//! For example, if we are comparing these:\n//! ```text\n//! BORROW:  (*x1[2].y).z.a\n//! ACCESS:  (*x1[i].y).w.b\n//! ```\n//!\n//! Then our steps are:\n//! ```text\n//!       x1         |   x1          -- places are the same\n//!       x1[2]      |   x1[i]       -- equal or disjoint (disjoint if indexes differ)\n//!       x1[2].y    |   x1[i].y     -- equal or disjoint\n//!      *x1[2].y    |  *x1[i].y     -- equal or disjoint\n//!     (*x1[2].y).z | (*x1[i].y).w  -- we are disjoint and don't need to check more!\n//! ```\n//!\n//! Because `zip` does potentially bad things to the iterator inside, this loop\n//! also handles the case where the access might be a *prefix* of the borrow, e.g.\n//!\n//! ```text\n//! BORROW:  (*x1[2].y).z.a\n//! ACCESS:  x1[i].y\n//! ```\n//!\n//! Then our steps are:\n//! ```text\n//!       x1         |   x1          -- places are the same\n//!       x1[2]      |   x1[i]       -- equal or disjoint (disjoint if indexes differ)\n//!       x1[2].y    |   x1[i].y     -- equal or disjoint\n//! ```\n//!\n//! -- here we run out of access - the borrow can access a part of it. If this\n//! is a full deep access, then we *know* the borrow conflicts with it. However,\n//! if the access is shallow, then we can proceed:\n//!\n//! ```text\n//!       x1[2].y    | (*x1[i].y)    -- a deref! the access can't get past this, so we\n//!                                     are disjoint\n//! ```\n//!\n//! Our invariant is, that at each step of the iteration:\n//!  - If we didn't run out of access to match, our borrow and access are comparable\n//!    and either equal or disjoint.\n//!  - If we did run out of access, the borrow can access a part of it.\n\nuse std::cmp::max;\nuse std::iter;\n\nuse rustc_hir as hir;\nuse rustc_middle::bug;\nuse rustc_middle::mir::{\n    Body, BorrowKind, FakeBorrowKind, MutBorrowKind, Place, PlaceElem, PlaceRef, ProjectionElem,\n};\nuse rustc_middle::ty::{self, TyCtxt};\nuse tracing::{debug, instrument};\n\nuse crate::{AccessDepth, ArtificialField, Deep, Overlap, Shallow};\n\n/// When checking if a place conflicts with another place, this enum is used to influence decisions\n/// where a place might be equal or disjoint with another place, such as if `a[i] == a[j]`.\n/// `PlaceConflictBias::Overlap` would bias toward assuming that `i` might equal `j` and that these\n/// places overlap. `PlaceConflictBias::NoOverlap` assumes that for the purposes of the predicate\n/// being run in the calling context, the conservative choice is to assume the compared indices\n/// are disjoint (and therefore, do not overlap).\n#[derive(Copy, Clone,
